Player_Black (to Proxy):

I’ll start by noting that my bishop on c8 is currently on the long diagonal (h3–g4–f5–e6–d7–c8) and is being “eyed” by White’s bishop sitting on h3. Since I can’t safely relocate the bishop (all its available moves lie on that same diagonal or are blocked), I must interpose a piece on one of those squares. One good resource is my knight on f6, which can legally jump to d7 (a (2,1) move) and land right in the middle of that diagonal. By playing ...Nf6d7, I block White’s bishop on h3 from directly attacking my bishop on c8. Furthermore, if White decides to capture my bishop on c8 afterward, I can recapture with the knight on d7, trading bishop for bishop on even terms.

With that in mind, my move is:

make_move f6d7

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I'll play the move that wins white’s queen. I notice that the black knight on c3 can jump to d1 (a legal knight move from c3 → d1, as the knight’s move from c3 is (±1, ±2) and d1 is exactly (1, –2) from c3). Since white’s queen is still sitting on d1 and no white piece besides the king can recapture (the white knight on b1 and the white bishop on c1 can’t reach d1 because other pieces block their paths), this capture forces white to recapture with the king. This exchange leaves white losing a queen for a knight—a decisive material gain for black.

So I’ll complete my move now.

make_move c3d1

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I'll develop my knight. My move is:

make_move c1b3

Here's my reasoning:

• The black knight sitting on c1 has four potential departures (to a2, b3, d3, or e2). Moving to d3 would capture the white pawn on d3, but that would likely let White recapture with the bishop from f1—trading a knight (worth roughly 3 points) for a pawn (1 point), which isn’t favorable.

• Moving to e2 is too risky, as it falls immediately under fire by the white bishop on f1.

• A knight retreat to a2 is rather passive and puts it on the rim, where it’s harder to redeploy.

• Therefore, moving the knight to b3 (c1→b3) is a natural developing move that brings the knight closer to the center without incurring immediate tactical liabilities.

Although White’s knight on g5 might later consider leaping to e6 attempting a fork of our queen on d8 and the bishop on f8, we have resources. In particular, if White’s knight goes to e6, our f7‐pawn will be ready to capture it. 

So I choose to play 1...Nc1b3.
